  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_1d6bcd73-48dd-4481-9fd1-b1ac44952d32'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer15806736775366435765">Perhaps the most ambitious novel from one of Mexico's greatest writers, the narrative covers 20 centuries of European and American culture, and prominently features the construction of El Escorial by Philip II. The title is Latin for "Our earth". Modeled on James Joyce's Finnegans Wake, Terra Nostra shifts unpredictably between the sixteenth century and the twentieth, seek</span>
  <span id="freeText15806736775366435765" style="display:none">Perhaps the most ambitious novel from one of Mexico's greatest writers, the narrative covers 20 centuries of European and American culture, and prominently features the construction of El Escorial by Philip II. The title is Latin for "Our earth". Modeled on James Joyce's Finnegans Wake, Terra Nostra shifts unpredictably between the sixteenth century and the twentieth, seeking the roots of contemporary Latin American society in the struggle between the conquistadors and indigenous Americans. <br /><br />-Terra Nostra is the spreading out of the novel, the exploration of its possibilities, the voyage to the edge of what only a novelist can see and say.- Milan Kundera</span>
  <a data-text-id="15806736775366435765" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_271750107" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/271750107" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/271750107?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=latin-america">latin-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=read-in-translation">read-in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=novel">novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer271750107" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15589840672155269431">I don't think I was the right reader for this book, it seems to have settled uneasily within me.<br/><br/>I am tempted to say the book is about politics and above all political forms. An alternative and ahistorical Philip II (married to Elizabeth of England) fights to impose his will and Catholic orthodoxy on the heterodox rebels of the Low Countries. The external politics is mirrored in his construction of El Escorial as an embodiment of the Orthodox unity he is trying to impose - however even this might</span>
  <span id="freeText15589840672155269431" style="display:none">I don't think I was the right reader for this book, it seems to have settled uneasily within me.<br /><br />I am tempted to say the book is about politics and above all political forms. An alternative and ahistorical Philip II (married to Elizabeth of England) fights to impose his will and Catholic orthodoxy on the heterodox rebels of the Low Countries. The external politics is mirrored in his construction of El Escorial as an embodiment of the Orthodox unity he is trying to impose - however even this mighty fortress proves not to be safe from heterodoxy.<br /><br />The figure of Philip is opposed by three six-figured brothers (among others) one of whom surfaces as a Casanova amongst the nuns serving in El Escorial. But all the opposition within time fails.<br /><br />The idea of opposition is broadened by the Old and New Worlds acting as mirrors to each other. The New World seems to offer to the characters the possibility of something alien to the tradition of Empire than is established in Europe, a tradition that has the Emperor Tiberius at one end and Philip II at the other trying to prevent change, to maintain orthodoxy (broadly envisioned) and by so doing maintaining power.<br /><br />Part of the challenge is overtly political and part through stories. Stories can be transgressive because narratives, we see, shape and form reality. This is demonstrated through the stories that a group of refugees from Spain (including the future Philip incognito) tell to each other. The political challenge is shown failing to overthrow the idea of Empire. Opposition then manifests itself in deviance as with the figure hiding among the nuns of El Escorial who then becomes the prototype of Don Juan. In this way we see how stories create counter narratives that embody resistance to the dominant Imperial power which in Fuentes' take asserts itself <i>ad astra</i>, not just in the political field but in all areas of human life.<br /><br />But even stories, including the one we are being told, can only triumph outside of time and so the end of the novel is set in 20th century Paris and is a moment of revolutionary eschatology. Humanity dies off amidst signs and wonders. The last man merges with the last woman in what might be the union of the Old World and the New and then makes love to itself. The figure of the last man (in this case a six fingered one, naturally) and the last woman might be a sign of how far Fuentes is going, the relative positions of men and women, the narratives we exist within of what men and women are and what each can do are themselves power structures, more immediate to most lives than the political structures exemplified by the figures of Tiberius and Philip II in this novel.<br /><br />It's a big novel and sweeps up a lot of material. A socio-political Arabian Nights, one story flowing into the next. Much of this is rich in allusion with deliberate contrasts and confirmations built into the story (Old World versus New, Philip II is a new Tiberius, each an individual as well as the exemplar of an attitude towards life). My problem is that it started to feel unconvincing, the kind of thing full of surface flash and fizz that is very exciting to read as a teenager but is in retrospect too obvious given the degrees of subtly possible in the novel. On the other hand it would make for a striking opera with ballet sequences. Or there again perhaps I'm simply too puritan and not imbued with the spirit of carnival to be a connoisseur of this novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15589840672155269431" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_37008645" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/37008645" />
    <a title="brian   " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/193310-brian"><img alt="brian   " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353195524p2/193310.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/37008645?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="brian   " class="user" itemprop="url" name="brian   " href="/user/show/193310-brian">brian   </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer37008645" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16462259780230444237">well, this is a big big book and i dig the big ones so i carried it around like a cinderblock in my bag for a while. and the first paragraph ranks as one of the great first paragraphs. check this out: <br/><br/><i>Incredible the first animal that dreamed of another animal. Monstrous the first vertebrae that succeeded in standing on two feet and thus spread terror among the beasts still normally and happily crawling close to the ground through the slime of creation. Astounding the first telephone call, the fi</i></span>
  <span id="freeText16462259780230444237" style="display:none">well, this is a big big book and i dig the big ones so i carried it around like a cinderblock in my bag for a while. and the first paragraph ranks as one of the great first paragraphs. check this out: <br /><br /><i>Incredible the first animal that dreamed of another animal. Monstrous the first vertebrae that succeeded in standing on two feet and thus spread terror among the beasts still normally and happily crawling close to the ground through the slime of creation. Astounding the first telephone call, the first boiling water, the first song, the first loincloth. </i><br /><br />holyfuckingshitamazing, huh? so i'm excited to get into this thing. and it's dark and weird and crazy and a story kicks in and it's gripping as shit. and then i get kinda bored. and then i hit part two and it's just phenomenal. i mean awesome. and then i get bored again. and then i stop halfway through. <br /><br />here's the deal: fuentes sets out to do no less than cover christian mythology and pre-cortesian mexican mythology -- to dramatize the birth and death of religions and civilizations. but fuentes is more concerned with ideas, it seems, than writing a great book; he places symbolism, allegory, theme, and idea before drama -- and with a book of this nature, that's a bad road taken. the best narrative fiction is that in which the great ideas are invisible, wriggling around inside the dramatic and the visceral and the real and the alive. it's all part of one thing, y'know?<br /><br />so i take the easy road and stamp this thing with 3 stars. <br /><br />yeah yeah, this kinda smashes my well earned reputation as an opinionated dickhead, but i'm still a little high from last night's victory. <br /><br />piss off. <br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2059576943" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2059576943" />
    <a title="Edward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12933866-edward"><img alt="Edward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444136022p2/12933866.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2059576943?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/12933866-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2059576943" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11077966413297310987"><i>Terra Nostra</i> is a dreamscape, an elaborate allegory, infused with mysticism, symbolism, numerology, theology. There are tales nested within tales, dreams within dreams, a mirror of our world distorted beyond reason, but not recognition. The linear is discarded in favour of the circular, the finite arc of human life replaced by an endless cycle of repetition. In this view of history as a singularity there is no Old World or New, no past or future, only man and his own fractal nature, which is at </span>
  <span id="freeText11077966413297310987" style="display:none"><i>Terra Nostra</i> is a dreamscape, an elaborate allegory, infused with mysticism, symbolism, numerology, theology. There are tales nested within tales, dreams within dreams, a mirror of our world distorted beyond reason, but not recognition. The linear is discarded in favour of the circular, the finite arc of human life replaced by an endless cycle of repetition. In this view of history as a singularity there is no Old World or New, no past or future, only man and his own fractal nature, which is at all times and places equally perverse and equally transcendent. The facets comprising the kaleidoscope of diverse cultural, religious and historical perspectives differ only arbitrarily, and from these arise repeated patterns that converge into a picture of a singular, essential human nature that transcends time and place. From this perspective the individual is reduced to archetype, one aspect of a super-organism which devours itself, growing larger only in order to devour itself still more. In its nature can be found the many ironies of existence: In the act of creation one must destroy; in conquest one becomes subjugated; in piety one is most corrupted. The thesis is encapsulated in the novel’s opening lines:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>Incredible the first animal that dreamed of another animal. Monstrous the first vertebrae that succeeded in standing on two feet and thus spread terror among the beasts still normally and happily crawling close to the ground through the slime of creation. Astounding the first telephone call, the first boiling water, the first song, the first loincloth.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Existence itself is seen as the primary cause of suffering. The nature of creation is such that to thrive is to destroy, and to prosper, obliterate. God becomes the ultimate destroyer: the twin of the creator god, to whom he is united by this fundamental contradiction. In the religion of Christianity it is not Adam who has committed the original sin, but God himself, in whose nature sin resides as an inseparable part of His virtue; a sin He commits by the very act of creation. Man too creates, and the crimes of man are a continuation of this shared nature, the dark half of the realisation of his destiny (though let us not forget that there is also a light half). It is perhaps no more enlightening to enquire as to why mankind seeks to commit acts of evil, than it is to ask why the waves seek to erode the shore.<br /><br />But the novel is not a nihilistic apology of conquest and colonialism, merely an examination of the nature that has caused this repeated fact of history, and the complex moral questions that arise from it. How can we exist as a moral species if the nature of life itself is that for one to rise, many more must fall? What forgotten price have others paid for our telephone calls, boiling water, songs and loincloths, and how can we but expect this cost to be paid again and again by the unlucky in perpetuity, as we proceed unchecked along this path? The example of thousands of years of human history does not point to a gradual abandonment of this modus operandi, but shows rather a species relentlessly striving to perfect it. Do we possess the ability to alter our course, or will the beast inevitably consume itself?</span>
  <a data-text-id="11077966413297310987" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

There is a well of intertextuality within which is nerdy yet effective.</span>

  <span id="freeText11647905146670217504" style="display:none">If <b>Hieronymus Bosch</b> were a writer…<br />“Behind the funeral coach follows a tortuous, writhing retinue of beggars, contrite, sobbing, swathed in dark rags, their mangy scabrous hands offering empty soup bowls to the dying sun; at times the most daring run ahead to beg a scrap of the rotten meat and are rewarded with kicks. But they are free to come and go, run ahead, fall behind.”<br />It isn’t just a funerary procession – it’s a pageant of history painted in sepulchral hues – a series of oneiric visions from the onset of Christianity to the dawn of Apocalypse.<br /><i>Terra Nostra</i> holds inside the entire cosmos of the modern literature and for me it remains one of the best books I ever read. <br /></span>

<div id="review_331027897" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/331027897" />
    <a title="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis"><img alt="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548295774p2/7607437.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer331027897" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12106047967225058404">Smell around this brick long enough (not long!) and you'll know whether you ought to read it. I know I did ;; and I did! And am better for it. But at any rate, wanted simply, in loo of a revieewooo, provide you with a non=representative passage which I'm sure many of you bookish folk will enjoy :::<br/><br/><br/>"You have named fifty stories, but you spoke of a thousand and one half days..."<br/>"Fifty accounts are accounts beyond count, Filipe. For from each account came twenty others, inopportunely, tempestuously</span>
  <span id="freeText12106047967225058404" style="display:none">Smell around this brick long enough (not long!) and you'll know whether you ought to read it. I know I did ;; and I did! And am better for it. But at any rate, wanted simply, in loo of a revieewooo, provide you with a non=representative passage which I'm sure many of you bookish folk will enjoy :::<br /><br /><br />"You have named fifty stories, but you spoke of a thousand and one half days..."<br />"Fifty accounts are accounts beyond count, Filipe. For from each account came twenty others, inopportunely, tempestuously, unseasonably, and each story contained as many others: the story told by the knight, the story lived by the knight, the story told to the knight, the story the knight read about himself in the press in Barcelona, the oral and anonymous version of the story told as pure verbal imminence before the knight existed, the version written in the papers of an Arabic chronicler, and based upon that, the version of a certain Cide Hamete; the version which to the knight's anger a shameless wretch by the name of Avellaneda had written apocryphally; the version the Squire Panza endlessly recounts to his wife, thus filling her to bursting with both intangible illusions and everyday proverbs; the version the priest tells the barber to kill the long hours in the village; and the version which to revive those same dead hours the barber tells the priest; the story as it is told by that frustrated writer, the bachelor Sanson Carrasco; the story that from his particular point of view Merlin the magician tells about those same events; the story the giants challenged by the knight tell among themselves, and the fantasy fabricated by the princesses whose spells he broke; the story told by Gines de Parapilla as part of his everlastingly unfinished memoirs; the one that Don Diego de Miranda, seeing it all from the viewpoint of friendship, set down in his diary; the story dreamed by Dulcinea, imagining herself a farm girl, and the story dreamed by the farm girl Aldonza, imagining herself a princess; and, finally, the story staged again and again, for the amusement of their court, by the Dukes in the theater of resurrections...."<br />"What did that maddened knight achieve by repeating to you twenty times each of his fifty adventures and all their version?"<br />"Simply the postponement of the day of judgment, which was to recover his sanity, lose his marvelous world, and die of scientific sadness."<br />"Then, in any case, he was defeated by destiny..."<br />"No, Felipe; in Barcelona we saw his adventures reproduced on paper, in hundreds and at times thousands of copies, thanks to a strange invention recently brought from Germany, which is a very rabbit of books: if you place a piece of paper in one mouth, from the other emerge ten, a hundred, a thousand, a million pages with the same letters..."<br />"Books reproduce themselves?"<br />"Yes, there is no longer a single copy, commissioned by you, written only for you, and illuminated by a monk, which you can keep in your library and reserve for your eyes alone." <br />"A thousand and one half days, you said, but you have accounted only for fifty stories in twenty versions: one half day is missing...."<br />"And will never be completed, Filipe. That half day is the infinite sum of the readers of this book, for as one finishes reading, one minute later another begins to read, and as that one finishes his reading, on minute later another begins it, and so on and so on, as in the ancient example of the hare and tortoise: Neither wins the race; so, too, the book si never without a reader, the book belongs to everyone..." <br />"Then, wretch that I am, reality belongs to everyone, for only what is written is real." [pp604f]</span>

<div id="review_91561179" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91561179" />
    <a title="Brent Hayward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2757350-brent-hayward"><img alt="Brent Hayward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494337604p2/2757350.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/91561179?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brent Hayward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brent Hayward" href="/user/show/2757350-brent-hayward">Brent Hayward</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer91561179" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer5342821977207208797">This pink brick was on the shelves of The Monkey's Paw, a store more likely to sell you a dusty stuffed crow or pornography from 1850's than some crazed scream from Carlos Fuentes about faith and death and history. I had wanted to read the book for a decade or so, snatching up the fat Penguin- an edition I'd never seen before- on my way back from the liquor store. 890 pages of size 3 font, three months, two countries, a 50th birthday, and several cities later, I won't even begin to address the c</span>
  <span id="freeText5342821977207208797" style="display:none">This pink brick was on the shelves of The Monkey's Paw, a store more likely to sell you a dusty stuffed crow or pornography from 1850's than some crazed scream from Carlos Fuentes about faith and death and history. I had wanted to read the book for a decade or so, snatching up the fat Penguin- an edition I'd never seen before- on my way back from the liquor store. 890 pages of size 3 font, three months, two countries, a 50th birthday, and several cities later, I won't even begin to address the contents. Except to say that they take place well in the past, and into the future, in Spain and in an alternate world, and that a good deal of the characters have six toes on each foot. And that there is much suffering, lurid sex, evil dictators, and betrayal. There's birth, too, more suffering, mad dwarves, and both the beginning and the end of time. This is the sort of book that make a writer want to throw in the pen. The parts when Terra Nostra falters are better than the best parts of other books. Just awesome, as the kids would say. Exhausting, humbling, and just fucking awesome. </span>

<div id="review_1399438874" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1399438874" />
    <a title="L.S. Popovich" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4567704-l-s-popovich"><img alt="L.S. Popovich" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1537455199p2/4567704.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1399438874?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="L.S. Popovich" class="user" itemprop="url" name="L.S. Popovich" href="/user/show/4567704-l-s-popovich">L.S. Popovich</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4567704-l-s-popovich?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4567704-l-s-popovich?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4567704-l-s-popovich?shelf=great-masterpieces">great-masterpieces</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4567704-l-s-popovich?shelf=monolithic-tomes">monolithic-tomes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4567704-l-s-popovich?shelf=spanish-language">spanish-language</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1399438874" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3265122761337781232">A Möbius striptease.<br/>Time is a permeable membrane.<br/>Cervantes and Caesar, Bosch and Quetzalcoatl.<br/>Historical figures rise, maggot-ridden from their tombs to conquer, make love, philosophize and dissolve in the polychromatic strobe of dreams. These fantasies fuse with antiquity, birthed from moldered tomes, exhausting the faiths of pious men, eviscerating kings, and bleeding across timelines.<br/><br/>The symbolic journey of this novel is an intense, dense, immense expedition through Old Spain, New Spain, and </span>
  <span id="freeText3265122761337781232" style="display:none">A Möbius striptease.<br />Time is a permeable membrane.<br />Cervantes and Caesar, Bosch and Quetzalcoatl.<br />Historical figures rise, maggot-ridden from their tombs to conquer, make love, philosophize and dissolve in the polychromatic strobe of dreams. These fantasies fuse with antiquity, birthed from moldered tomes, exhausting the faiths of pious men, eviscerating kings, and bleeding across timelines.<br /><br />The symbolic journey of this novel is an intense, dense, immense expedition through Old Spain, New Spain, and lands beyond, fraught with wordplay, wigwams, and wampeters. The repetitions, revolutions, and rhythms blossom in the final pages, recalling the mythological wheel of time, the mechanics of Fate, God playing 'ghost in the machine,' and ouroboroses in a boudoir. As Kundera explains in his afterword, the novel spreads its wings to encompass interior and exterior worlds, landscapes of the mind and the abyss of the heart. <br /><br />A novel of conquest, submission, doom, and the many frightened cries of the powerless souls lost in the continuous apocalypse of the past. The past rests on our shoulders, like a prolapsed soul, weighty, invasive, and recurrent.<br /><br />The Nature of existence, echoing the edenic ambitions human beings inherit from the great puppeteer in the cosmic theater. A bold deathly pale specter hovering over Mexican literature, this monolithic masterpiece bends your ear gently, only to scream its nightmarish hymn into the echo chamber of your brain.<br /><br />An unforgettable, Joycean whirlpool of perennial, Imperialist themes, set to a constant boil until the precipitate becomes a Kraken with its myriad limbs straddling the limits of temporal awareness and physical sensation.<br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3639390348" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3639390348" />
    <a title="Tobias" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34694041-tobias"><img alt="Tobias" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573238082p2/34694041.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3639390348?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tobias" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tobias" href="/user/show/34694041-tobias">Tobias</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3639390348" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1707198904505303340"><b>Monstrous virtuosity, an expanding universe, world without end... </b><br/><br/>Reading Carlos Fuentes’ Terra Nostra sometimes feels like gazing at the Sistine Chapel, or one of Bosch’s triptychs. The sheer amount of and attention to detail overwhelms the singularity of its creation, to the point where only the mosaic itself is visible. It appears almost inhuman and alien, out of bounds for human creativity, a novel driven by the same force that drove Dante’s Ulysses beyond the pillars of Hercules. But readin</span>
  <span id="freeText1707198904505303340" style="display:none"><b>Monstrous virtuosity, an expanding universe, world without end... </b><br /><br />Reading Carlos Fuentes’ Terra Nostra sometimes feels like gazing at the Sistine Chapel, or one of Bosch’s triptychs. The sheer amount of and attention to detail overwhelms the singularity of its creation, to the point where only the mosaic itself is visible. It appears almost inhuman and alien, out of bounds for human creativity, a novel driven by the same force that drove Dante’s Ulysses beyond the pillars of Hercules. But reading it again, slowly, and I mean very slowly, the seams stitching everything together emerge; the flawlessness of its design is vastly overshadowed by the intricacy of its composite elements. <br /><br />Part historical novel and part mythological retelling of the Spanish Empire, Terra Nostra is a chaosmos which uncovers a Viconian cycle of history with El Escorial as its birthplace and its grave, but it is a novel that cannot be reduced to a simple cycle of viciousness; tragically so. Let me explain.<br /><br />At the beginning of the end of the third of the book’s three parts, we find an old knight with creaking bones and a broken sword inside a windmill. He was not always this way, he tells us, he was in fact once young and in love, but his beloved Dulcinea was killed by her father and he in turn by our Knight of the Sorrowful Countenance. He fled, he confesses, to a village whose name he does not wish to recall,<br /><br />“’alone, knowing in my flesh the truth of what the old procuress who furnished me Dulcinea’s favors had told me: old age is a hostelry of illness, an inn for thoughts, unremitting anguish, incurable wound, stain of the past, pain of the present, morose concern for the future, neighbor to death, and, standing as tall as possible in the cart and raising his lance as if to wound the clouds, he said, books were my only consolation, I read them all, I imagined I could be one of those flawless knights, rescue those illustrious ladies, vanquish those perfidious giants and magicians...”<br /><br />He returns to the gravestone of his beloved and her father, wishing to make amends, but instead of forgiveness he is met by a curse. The statue of Dulcinea’s father condemns him to something worse than hell, that his reading would become reality, that his fragile body would actually face giants and monsters, only visible to him, to be mocked, caged, taken for a madman. <br /><br />The knight accepts the curse. He fights monsters disguised as windmills and armies dressed as sheep until his squire abandons him: <br /><br />“But see what my fate has been, sirs, that I see the truth of things which others hold to be a lie; the enchantment lay upon the others; and greater the enchantment of my enchantment, as I saw that only I, cursed by the statue of Dulcinea’s father, saw giants where others, as if enchanted, saw only windmills [...]. But do you know what my revenge will be? I shall declare that my reason has returned. I shall keep my secret. I shall accept that everything I have seen is a lie. I shall try to convince no one.”<br /><br />To Fuentes, fiction may very well be a mirror of the world, but a mirror which only reflects back darkly. Fuentes demands of his readers to consider the blasphemous implications of reading itself; to imagine a world of fiction as boundless as reality but constrained within the limitations of the pages. Reality is a sick dream, as he writes elsewhere, but if that it so, then reading is a nightmare without end. Fuentes’ triptych is a landscape in which<br /><br />“anything is possible; nothing must be rejected; nature, and in particular human nature, encompasses each and every level of existence, from the divine to the diabolic, from the bestil to the mystic; nothing is beyond belief; nothing is beyond possibility; the only possibilities we deny are the possibilities we do not know...”<br /><br />What possibilities, say? A baby girl born in the stable of Bethlehem, Pilate condemning Barabbas to die on the cross, Socrates refusing the temptation of suicide, an Odysseus who never made it out of Troy, the Trojan horse consumed by flames, Helen returned home, Job fleeing his, Abel forgotten by Cain, Antigone submitting to the law, Lucifer restored, Noah’s ark sinking. No, one cycle is already too much to bear.<br /><br />It is a novel that covers so much material that it seems suspicious, if not downright implausible, that it can be contained within the constricts of a book bound in paper and glue. But then again, as El Senor repeats over and over, we must trust only what is written. I stand by my original statement, that this novel is not just one of the greatest of the twentieth century, a century of so many great novels, but one of the greatest in human history, a virtuoso at the peak of his powers constructing, stone for stone, one monstrous theology of brutality. Please read this; it won't make you a better person, but if it doesn't make you a better reader then you're reading wrong.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1707198904505303340" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2308839987" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2308839987" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3143117-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571421692p2/3143117.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2308839987?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/3143117-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9786071120519" href="/book/show/17174317-terra-nostra">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3143117-david?shelf=espa%C3%B1ol">español</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3143117-david?shelf=mexican-lit">mexican-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2308839987" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10037550663492350795">Stop me if you have heard this before?<br/><br/>A Mexican writer walks into the hotel bar. He joins his fellow Latin Americans, an Argentinian, a Chilean, a Peruvian and a Colombian (Julio Cortázar, Jose Donoso, Mario Vargas Llosa and Gabriel Garcia Marquez) and says “ — Todos los buenos latinoamericanos vienen a morir a Paris.” (Every good Latin American comes to Paris to die). They all laugh in agreement.<br/><br/>The date is December 31, 1999. The end of the Millenium. The world is ravaged by microbes causing wo</span>
  <span id="freeText10037550663492350795" style="display:none">Stop me if you have heard this before?<br /><br />A Mexican writer walks into the hotel bar. He joins his fellow Latin Americans, an Argentinian, a Chilean, a Peruvian and a Colombian (Julio Cortázar, Jose Donoso, Mario Vargas Llosa and Gabriel Garcia Marquez) and says “ — Todos los buenos latinoamericanos vienen a morir a Paris.” (Every good Latin American comes to Paris to die). They all laugh in agreement.<br /><br />The date is December 31, 1999. The end of the Millenium. The world is ravaged by microbes causing world starvation and there is an order out to curb the population growth. The Mexicans have brought back the fight to the death Aztec ball game shown on the screens. The Russians distribute copies of the works of Trotsky and will shoot them if they are caught reading the books. In Río de Janeiro the military orders a perpetual carnaval so people can party themselves to death. The French decide their fates through debates on television. Ah, the French are so civilized.<br /><br />A very distopian story. But the only continuous thing playing on the screens is a show about the 16th century Spanish king Philip II. And the bulk of the enormous 950-page book centres around Felipe (known as El Señor), son of Felipe, El hermoso and Joanna Regina (La Dama Loca) and married to the English queen Isabel (La Señora). The cast runs some three pages and includes his friends, known as los soñadores or “The Dreamers,” who are friends of the young king and spans some twenty years. <br /><br />Yep, a soap opera.<br /><br />Why Felipe II? Felipe was the king of the Spanish empire. He didn’t want to leave Spain, and for the most part, neither left Escorial, the tomb-like palace he built for himself and, according to the story, didn’t put much faith in the new world, even though so much money came in it. Sadly to finance his plans, he borrowed to the hilt and often defaulted. He claimed that the Christians, Jews and Muslims lived in harmony but also chased them out of Spain. He claimed that he never touched his English wife and yet both relied heavily on lovers. In other words, a soap opera.<br /><br />The book has moments of brilliance and utter shear beauty in the language. There were times when passages went on too long and I thought he was rambling. The characters are all flawed and that makes them worthy of reading. There is a description of the Garden of Earthly Delights by Hieronymus Bosch that is breathtaking. <br /><br />So why did Fuentes write this book? Perhaps to say that the conquistadors left Spain to conquer a new world and once there, the same corrupt ways corrupted the new world. Felipe asked one of his conquistadors if he found paradise? No, only hell. And as the dystopian world collapsed in 1999, reflecting on the past meant seeing everything come full circle. Have we really advanced or are we truly victims of history repeating itself. The Spanish rule sure doesn’t bode well, especially from a Mexican point of view.<br /><br />Published in 1975, Fuentes tackles magic realism, time travel and assorted tricks, like including himself and his colleagues in the story. Time seems irrelevant and yet, very relevant. He even includes a story of the Roman emperor Tiberius, covering two thousand years.<br /><br />Did I like it? Yes, no and everything in between. Why so long I asked? And yet as I neared the end, I realized that I would miss the book. That was a very odd feeling.<br /><br />I have read most of Fuentes’ books (and he wrote a lot) and not even sure where to put it. I read an old New York Times review that says if you are going to fail, fail big. I wouldn’t put this down to failure. Epic? Massive? Yes. Very good at least. Very long. One month to read. Yes. Very big.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10037550663492350795" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2308839987">

<div id="review_746058627" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/746058627" />
    <a title="Marc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5962442-marc"><img alt="Marc" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485288542p2/5962442.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/746058627?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc" href="/user/show/5962442-marc">Marc</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer746058627" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5373254002620864291">Utch, this was a strange one, and one of those books I really hoped I would have liked more then I actually did. <br/><br/>It's certainly ambitious, insanely so, and takes some very interesting liberties with narrative space and time. It's also refreshingly aggressive and non-subtle in it's attack on religion and power in general, and quite entertaining too, at least most of the time. Fuentes furthermore seems to have a great love of the grotesque (bodily mutilations and repulsive sex scenes galore) combi</span>
  <span id="freeText5373254002620864291" style="display:none">Utch, this was a strange one, and one of those books I really hoped I would have liked more then I actually did. <br /><br />It's certainly ambitious, insanely so, and takes some very interesting liberties with narrative space and time. It's also refreshingly aggressive and non-subtle in it's attack on religion and power in general, and quite entertaining too, at least most of the time. Fuentes furthermore seems to have a great love of the grotesque (bodily mutilations and repulsive sex scenes galore) combined with a very dark and morbid sense of humor. <br /><br />All good, but unfortunately the novel is also far to long and overwritten, with characters constantly breaking out in long circular soliloquies, and endless stretches of some sort of collective stream-of-consciousness. Now, as a fan of McElroy, Pynchon, Vollmann etc. I have no problems in general with long books and/or these sort of narrative tactics, but Fuentes just doesn't pull it off quite convincingly. To make matters worse, he also seems to not really trust the readers ability to follow the complex narrative, and so fills the novel with repetition and reminders, in case one should have forgotten what happened 100 pages ago, which - apart from not helping with the length - felt somewhat condescending to at least this reader.<br /><br />Still: It's not a very hard read, and when it good, it's <i>good</i>, so if you like slightly flawed experiments, and don't mind being a bit bored once in a while, give it a go.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5373254002620864291" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_20062352" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/20062352" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1062920-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1207586604p2/1062920.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/20062352?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/1062920-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer20062352" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14893634943315581518">Epic and kaleidoscopic in scope, full of profound weirdness and stunning, hallucinatory prose. Forget Garcia-Marquez, this is more Pynchonian in its lucid irrationality, a waking dream of Spain's conquest of Mexico that straddles multiple centuries, from Aztec creation myth to Millenial apocalypse. Alternately frustrating and mind-blowing - I came close to quitting it more than once, particularly in the first book, "The Old World," but Fuentes kept dragging me back with his wild imagination and </span>
  <span id="freeText14893634943315581518" style="display:none">Epic and kaleidoscopic in scope, full of profound weirdness and stunning, hallucinatory prose. Forget Garcia-Marquez, this is more Pynchonian in its lucid irrationality, a waking dream of Spain's conquest of Mexico that straddles multiple centuries, from Aztec creation myth to Millenial apocalypse. Alternately frustrating and mind-blowing - I came close to quitting it more than once, particularly in the first book, "The Old World," but Fuentes kept dragging me back with his wild imagination and elegant prose. The second book, "The New World," stands on its own as an epic re-imagining of Mexico's origins and conquest. And the third book, "The Next World," is just a complete mind-bender, with side trips to ancient Rome and a savage version of modern Mexico, where human sacrifice has been reinstated and dissent is suppressed by the US military. A dark &amp; twisted masterpiece from the lion of Mexican lit.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14893634943315581518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_38801962" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/38801962" />
    <a title="Alex V." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/334560-alex-v"><img alt="Alex V." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442608235p2/334560.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/38801962?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex V." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex V." href="/user/show/334560-alex-v">Alex V.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/334560-alex-v?shelf=didn-t-finish-it">didn-t-finish-it</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer38801962" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17977566553326277856"><i>Terra Nostra</i> has the most profound opening paragraph of any book this side of The Bible:<br/><br/><i>Incredible the animal that first dreamed of another animal. Monstrous the first vertebrate that succeeded in standing on two feet and thus spread terror among the beasts still normally and happily crawling close to the ground through the slime of creation. Astounding the first telephone call, the first boiling water, the first song, the first loincloth.</i><br/><br/>and then shortly after there, Fuentes lost me. Or, rather</span>
  <span id="freeText17977566553326277856" style="display:none"><i>Terra Nostra</i> has the most profound opening paragraph of any book this side of The Bible:<br /><br /><i>Incredible the animal that first dreamed of another animal. Monstrous the first vertebrate that succeeded in standing on two feet and thus spread terror among the beasts still normally and happily crawling close to the ground through the slime of creation. Astounding the first telephone call, the first boiling water, the first song, the first loincloth.</i><br /><br />and then shortly after there, Fuentes lost me. Or, rather, I got lost. This is a huge cosmic book of dense interwoven prose with at least one line as good as the opening paragraph tucked in the folds of each of its 700+ pages. I wish I had the time to sit at it like an eager undergrad, newly acclimated to putting a highlighter to a weathered copy weighing down my backpack, extracting things-to-quote from its thicket, desecrating a weathered copy with my love and devotion, chuckling to myself openly and brazenly about "Fuentes", forlornly wishing there was that one someone with whom to share that one line I'd found that day, and if that person found the line as profound as I, we would be soulmates. <br /><br />I am not that eager undergrad anymore, and I know the bonds of a good line are short-lived in the the actual <i>terra nostra</i>. The actual <i>terra nostra</i> is filled to capacity with good lines, spilling over the edge, likely forming the Abyss we furtively skirt with their radiating heat. And I have a soulmate who has her own well-sharpened sense of literature. And I don't have the time nor really the inclination to clear my calendar to make the time that this book deserves. So I will put this one aside, kidding myself to think I am richer for having spend a quick weekend in Fuentes' world, and nurse daydreams that I will one day return for a longer visit.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17977566553326277856" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2810274843" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2810274843" />
    <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera"><img alt="Inderjit Sanghera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564826235p2/900800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2810274843?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inderjit Sanghera" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera">Inderjit Sanghera</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2810274843" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2223409519375336248">Fuentes’s sprawling phantasmagoria acts as an exploration of Spain in the midst of its golden age and on the brink of discovering the new world; a world which replete leaves the reader ensorcelled by the hypnagogic beauty of the imagery which Fuentes conjures up;<br/><br/>“The shipwrecked youth believed he had been embalmed by the sea; blood pounded at his temples; he squinted through half-opened eyes; the sight of this fog-shrouded desert was perhaps not too different from what he would have discovered a</span>
  <span id="freeText2223409519375336248" style="display:none">Fuentes’s sprawling phantasmagoria acts as an exploration of Spain in the midst of its golden age and on the brink of discovering the new world; a world which replete leaves the reader ensorcelled by the hypnagogic beauty of the imagery which Fuentes conjures up;<br /><br />“The shipwrecked youth believed he had been embalmed by the sea; blood pounded at his temples; he squinted through half-opened eyes; the sight of this fog-shrouded desert was perhaps not too different from what he would have discovered at the floor of an ocean of fire, for as he fell from the ship’s forecastle to the sea, he couldn’t see the waves he was falling toward, only the blazing corposant above him”<br /><br />And so the world which Fuentes creates shimmers and refracts in the eyes of the reader as if in a dream, as we meet the court of King Felipe and become embroiled in the intrigue and cruelty which dominates his court, all whilst the nascent possibilities of the new world loom over the characters. In truth Fuentes’s style is a little uneven and the fantastical stories of travellers about South America form some of the weakest part of the book, being burdened by fables and allegories to the point of being somewhat disjointed against the backdrop of the novel.<br /><br />But perhaps then again that is the point behind Fuentes’s novel, a novel which has constant jumps in time, form the new millennium to the first, from Paris to Jerusalem to the acrid and arid Spanish deserts or febrile coast, from the life of a licentious and quite literal Don Juan to a capricious king. Fuentes’s novel is, at times hard to read, but its startling originality, both in terms of plot and prose more than makes up for this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2223409519375336248" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2241649490" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2241649490" />
    <a title="Vicky Hunt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10310364-vicky-hunt"><img alt="Vicky Hunt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563124889p2/10310364.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2241649490?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vicky Hunt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vicky Hunt" href="/user/show/10310364-vicky-hunt">Vicky Hunt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10310364-vicky-hunt?shelf=spanish-translations">spanish-translations</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2241649490" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15112359076778616848"><b>Like reading Shakespeare as your first English Language Novel</b><br/><br/>Terra Nostra, written by Carlos Fuentes and almost a Spanish classic, is Metaphor rich, like existing in a living dream. There are sweeping panoramic changes in view. And, actual events are taken out of their historical sequence and combined with phantasmagoric events. With the use of the pronoun ‘Our’ in Our Earth (the title) – Fuentes is speaking directly to and about the Hispanic world.<br/><br/>In the opening sequence, a young man named Poll</span>
  <span id="freeText15112359076778616848" style="display:none"><b>Like reading Shakespeare as your first English Language Novel</b><br /><br />Terra Nostra, written by Carlos Fuentes and almost a Spanish classic, is Metaphor rich, like existing in a living dream. There are sweeping panoramic changes in view. And, actual events are taken out of their historical sequence and combined with phantasmagoric events. With the use of the pronoun ‘Our’ in Our Earth (the title) – Fuentes is speaking directly to and about the Hispanic world.<br /><br />In the opening sequence, a young man named Pollo falls into the River Seine and washes ashore on the beach of 16th Century Spain as a different character. Though it opens there in Paris, France, the shift to Spain is the locale of most of the action in the book. Some of it takes place in pre-Columbian Mexico, and some in the time of Tiberius Caesar in the first century AD. These places can be seen in the named parts of the book; The Old World, The New World, and The Next World. <br /><br /><br />The novel is truly Iberian in its nature; encompassing Spain and all of Latin American culture in its plot. But, there seems to be a huge chasm between the two (besides the Atlantic.) The Old World and the New World are seen as separate worlds. And, then there is the world he sees in the future. Fuentes believes that all Hispanic cultural and political history can be summed up in the 16th Century Spanish Empire. In El Escorial Palace &amp; Monastery we see the fact expressed both literally in the book and physically in the architecture that the Church and State live together in the Spanish world. He thinks the Latin world diverged in the discovery of the New World, yet in the future they will join in harmony.<br /><br /><br />Fuentes often uses a staccato rhythm of writing in a pleasing manner: <br /><blockquote>“Silvery webs in gardens and pantheons. Gilded webs on the smooth ceiling of the apartment in the Hôtel du Pont-Royal. The red suite. Flaming velvet. Outside, the snow is a melted standard and the river the lion rampant of the banner. Inside, white stucco. Vines. Cornucopias. Cherubs. Plaster sculpture. Red velvet and white plaster. Mirrors. Stained. Spotted with age.”</blockquote> <br /><br /><br />It is close to impossible to form an opinion of Fuentes’ ability to construct characters from Terra Nostra alone, for he seems to have not created a single character in this book. Instead he uses a number of actual historical characters, depicted in an askew manner to represent archetypes. The collage of images presented in each of the major characters makes every single one seem schizophrenic, and they are, because like actors in a low-budget play, no one portrays a single person.<br /><br /><br />The most important historical persons: <br />•	Felipe the Fair, Archduke Philip of Austria, King Philip I of Spain for several months before his death (1478-1506)<br />•	Felipe, also called El Señor, Philip II the king of Spain from 1556 to 1598 (Lived 1527-1598),<br />•	Isabel called La Señora, Elizabeth I (1533-1603), queen of England from 1558 to 1603 <br />•	Joanna Regina (1479-1555) the widow of Felipe the Fair<br />•	Tiberius Caesar, the emperor of Rome from 14 to 37 c.e<br /><br />The main characters of Terra Nostra are Felipe, his secretary Guzmán, his wife, Isabel, and his mother, Joanna, the Mad Lady. El Senor, Felipe, wants to live forever, but he doesn’t want to accept change. The Mad Lady (his Mother) seems to fear change. His wife seems to try to seize the day, living only in the moment.<br /><br /><br />But, these characters are not all. If you have watched the first Shrek movie (Disney) then you understand how the movie combines references to a multitude of pre-existing characters from children’s tales. Fuentes uses this same tactic, introducing a variety of characters and authors from Hispanic literature - even Cervantes is used. I especially enjoyed the 4 characters playing cards near the end, though I’d not read their books. They seem to be arguing over which one had the best/worst hand dealt to them, as a comedic twist. <br /><br /><br />And, here is where Fuentes excels in this book. His ability to ‘hold the mirror’ before his reader in a manner similar to the Biblical prophets makes you wonder if he’s having a laugh at your expense. Regardless of whether he’s laughing or not, he seems to have an almost bleak view, despite the harmony of worlds with which he ends. Maybe it is the past that he turns to throughout this tome that is his shelter from the messed up world he sees us living in today. He definitely sees time as cyclic.<br /><br /><br /><blockquote>Martians and Venusians have not invaded us, rather heretics and monks from the fifteenth century, conquistadors and painters from the sixteenth century, poets and entrepreneurs from the seventeenth century, philosophers, and revolutionaries from the eighteenth century, courtesans and social climbers from the nineteenth century: we have been occupied by the past.</blockquote><br /><br /><br />Fuentes presented the Hispanic world in a saltshaker of sorts. From this saltshaker, he sprinkled out the Roman Empire, the Spanish Empire, and the pre-Columbian Mexicans in the jungles of the Americas. He makes an attempt to end in harmony in the city of Lights… Paris. It’s tempting to ask why Fuentes ends in Paris. But, that is really the wrong question, because the real question is why he began in Paris. But, the Latin American problems with overpopulation and death can be implied from the metaphorically rich scenes that take place in Paris in the beginning and end of Fuentes’ novel. <br /><br /><br />As to the question of why Fuentes chose Paris for the start and end of the book. His answer is finally revealed in the last chapter.<br /><br /><br /><blockquote>Perhaps Paris was the exact moral, sexual, and intellectual point of balance between the two worlds that tear us apart: the Germanic and the Mediterranean, the North and the South, the Anglo-Saxon and the Latin.</blockquote><br /><br /><br />But, more than that, Fuentes wants the Hispanic world to see itself, like looking into a mirror. He uses this metaphor of a mirror quite a bit. And, of course he points to all the mirrors in Paris.<br /><br /><br /><blockquote>Paris seems much larger than it really is because of the infinite number of mirrors that duplicate its true space: Paris is Paris, plus its mirrors.</blockquote><br /><br /><br />Should you read this monumental work? <br />Terra Nostra is a great work, definitely and Fuentes is a genius to have come up with so much metaphor in such a scope of history. But, because of that there are constraints you should be aware of up front before deciding whether it is for you. I got sucked in first by the incredible narrator's voice of Walter Krochmal. It sounds incredible to listen to the man speak! But, I splurged to add Kindle e-text whisper-sync, because you definitely need to see the words to follow the ideas. <br /><br /><br />Also, you will most likely find it necessary to back-read some of the history of the events that take place in the book to 'get' the metaphors he dishes out in heavy doses. Being a North American, having some events turned into metaphor and changing actual historical events (like having Elizabeth marry Philip) can cause you to do a double-take in surprise at first. <br /><br /><br />Thirdly, the book can be quite graphic and explicit in some of its metaphor. To give an example of both the tame and the explicit, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I felt the beginning thread of the old lady giving birth, devolving in a dream like sequence of all the women of France giving birth on the streets to be a tame and intriguing metaphor for the birth of the New World. A bit later though, 'El Senor' finds some really disgusting things in his bed upon awaking, and thinks God brought that upon him, when actually his enemies brought an animal to give birth in his bed while he was passed out. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br />So, yes... a bit graphic at points and shocking in some of the metaphors. It reveals some of the extremes of the Spanish Catholic mindset of that era. And, it took me almost 2 months to finish it, since I read it at a slow pace. It is only about 45 or so hours of actual reading, but re-reading parts, and back-reading to ‘get it’ added to the research time makes it quite an investment of time. And, it is my first of Fuentes works. Being such a complex novel, reading it first of Fuentes’ books is like choosing Shakespeare for your first English language novel. But, I'm glad I discovered it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15112359076778616848" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
